[3:1:66,78] recycling fraud, officer-involved shootings, homicides, foreign prosecution, and human trafficking and sexual predator  

[3:2:66,88] apprehension crimes. In addition, BI serves a principal role in providing leadership, coordination, and support to law  

[4:0:66,98] enforcement through intelligence support centers and multi-agency task forces focusing on violent offenders, gangs, and major  

[4:1:66,108] crimes statewide.  

[5:0:66,127] The Office of the Chief (Office) provides administrative support to the investigative, regulatory, and forensic programs within the  

[5:1:66,137] Division of Law Enforcement and other criminal justice agencies. The Office serves as the policy-making and oversight body for  

[5:2:66,147] its four operational bureaus. The Office also includes the Office of Gun Violence Prevention (OGVP) to support the DOJ’s  

[5:3:66,156] ongoing efforts to reduce and prevent gun violence, firearm injury, and related trauma. 

[5:4:66,174] 0445 - CALIFORNIA JUSTICE INFORMATION SERVICES  

[6:0:66,193] The California Justice Information Services Division provides criminal justice intelligence, information, and identification  

[6:1:66,203] services to law enforcement, regulatory agencies, and the public. Four major functional areas carry out these primary services:  

[6:2:66,213] (1) The Justice Data and Investigative Services Bureau offers investigative and field services functions to criminal justice,  

[6:3:66,223] public safety, and regulatory agencies. Information and technical assistance are provided on a variety of manual and  

[6:4:66,232] automated systems, including the Stop Data Collection System, the Controlled Substance Utilization Review and Evaluation  

[6:5:66,242] System, the Criminal Justice Statistics Center, the California Sex and Arson Registry, and other person and property databases  

[6:6:66,252] used daily by state, local, and federal law enforcement agencies; (2) The Bureau of Criminal Information and Analysis  

[6:7:66,262] consolidates the functions related to biometric services housed in the California Identification System for retaining and  

[6:8:66,271] positively identifying finger, palm, and latent images; as well as, storage and dissemination of state summary criminal history  

[6:9:66,281] information for law enforcement and regulatory purposes; (3) The Hawkins Data Center operates the Criminal Justice  

[6:10:66,291] Information System and the California Law Enforcement Telecommunications System, as well as provides IT services for the  

[6:11:66,301] Division of Law Enforcement and the Attorney General’s Office; (4) the Operations Support Program provides business  

[6:12:66,311] resumption planning and administrative support and oversight.  

[6:13:66,328] 9900 - DIRECTORATE AND ADMINISTRATION  

[7:0:66,345] The Directorate and the Administration Division of the Department of Justice consists of the Attorney General's Executive  

[7:1:66,355] Office and the Division of Operations. The Executive Office maintains the overall direction and administration of the diverse  

[7:2:66,365] programs and projects of the department, including the Equal Employment Rights and Resolution Office, the Opinions Unit, the  

[7:3:66,374] Solicitor General's Office, the Office of Legislative Affairs, the Office of Communications, the Public Inquiry Unit, and the Office  

[7:4:66,384] of Community Awareness, Response, and Engagement. In addition, the Division of Operations provides support functions  

[7:5:66,394] essential to the Department's operations, including fiscal, personnel, and specialized business services such as legal  

[7:6:66,404] secretarial support, litigation support, legal case management services, and facilities management.

[11:0:66,705] The State Controller is the chief fiscal officer of California. Principally responsible for transparency and accountability of the  

[12:0:66,714] state's financial resources, the Controller ensures the appropriate disbursement and tracking of taxpayer dollars. The Controller

# === PAGE 166 === [size: 612x792]
[0:0:66,13] 2024-25 STATE BUDGET — LJE   164 

[1:0:382,13] LEGISLATIVE, JUDICIAL, AND EXECUTIVE 

[2:0:207,32] 0840   State Controller - Continued 

[3:0:66,68] serves on dozens of state boards, commissions, and committees with duties   ranging from   administrative oversight of the  

[4:0:66,78] nation's two largest public pension funds,   to protection of state lands and coastlines,   to modernization and financing of major  

[4:1:66,88] infrastructure. The Controller also offers fiscal guidance to local governments and has independent auditing authority over  

[4:2:66,98] government agencies related to the spending of state funds. The Controller's primary objectives are to:  

[5:0:65,115] • Account for and control disbursement of all state funds, issuing warrants in payment of the state's bills.  

[5:1:65,131] • Determine legality and accuracy of financial claims against the state.  

[5:2:65,147] • Audit state and local government programs and agencies.  

[6:0:65,162] • Ensure businesses report and remit unclaimed property in compliance with Unclaimed Property Law and safeguard remitted  

[6:1:76,172] unclaimed property until claimed by the rightful owners or heirs.

[5:0:66,226] PROGRAM DESCRIPTIONS  

[6:0:66,245] 0500100 - ACCOUNTING AND REPORTING  

[7:0:66,264] The State Accounting and Reporting Division maintains uniform and systematic control accounts of all receipts, payments,  

[7:1:66,274] appropriations, state fund balances, and bonded indebtedness; monitors the cash flow of the General Fund; administers the  

[7:2:66,284] statewide discharge from accountability program; participates in offsetting monies owed to the state; reports the financial  

[7:3:66,294] condition of the state; and collaborates with the Department of Finance, the State Treasurer, and the Department of General  

[7:4:66,303] Services to develop and implement the Financial Information System for California (FI$Cal) to ensure the system meets the  

[7:5:66,313] state's primary objectives and provides best business practices for the state.  

[8:0:66,333] The Local Government Programs and Services Division maintains a database of information and reports on local financial  

[8:1:66,343] transactions and government compensation; apportions shared revenues to local governments; administers local mandated  

[8:2:66,352] and non-mandated cost reimbursement programs; approves county cost allocation plans; prescribes uniform accounting  

[8:3:66,362] procedures for counties, special districts, and trial court revenues; plans and facilitates an annual conference with county  

[8:4:66,372] auditors; administers and collects estate and inheritance taxes; collects delinquent insurance and motor vehicle fuel taxes,  

[8:5:66,382] petroleum and gas assessments, and trial court penalties; refunds gasoline taxes paid on fuel consumed for off-highway  

[8:6:66,391] purposes; instructs and advises county tax collectors; approves state and local government requests to participate in the  

[8:7:66,401] Interagency Intercept Collection Program; collects recorder fees from counties pursuant to the Building Homes and Jobs Act,  

[8:8:66,411] (Chapter 364, Statutes of 2017); and administers the Property Tax Postponement Program for seniors and people who are  

[8:9:66,421] blind or have a disability.  

[9:0:66,438] 0500200 - AUDITS  

[10:0:66,458] The Audits Division determines the legality and accuracy of all claims against the state through the performance of prepayment  

[10:1:66,467] audits; assures the accuracy of local government claims and financial statements submitted to the state and federal  

[10:2:66,477] governments by establishing and updating audit guidelines, and reviewing audits performed by independent auditors; performs  

[10:3:66,487] field audits for state and federal programs; reviews the single audit reports of local governments and local education agencies  

[10:4:66,497] that receive pass-through federal funds from state agencies; and audits major businesses for compliance with the Unclaimed  

[10:5:66,506] Property Law.  

[11:0:66,524] 0500300 - PERSONNEL AND PAYROLL SERVICES  

[12:0:66,551] The Personnel and Payroll Services Division (PPSD) processes payroll and leave accounting for all state civil service and  

[12:1:66,560] exempt employees, state court, and California State University employees. PPSD provides information required to manage the  

[12:2:66,570] personnel resources of the state and to properly account for salary and wage expenditures, provides data to the retirement  

[12:3:66,580] systems necessary for calculation of employee retirement benefits, and manages the state’s automated travel expense  

[12:4:66,590] reimbursement system.  

[13:0:66,609] Cal Employee Connect (CEC) and ConnectHR are SCO’s secure web-based customer service portals for state employees and  

[13:1:66,619] human resources staff, respectively. They were developed as interim solutions to provide some of the enhancements the  

[13:2:66,629] California State Payroll System Project (CSPS) will deliver when completed, to support a hybrid remote workforce and make  

[13:3:66,639] personnel and payroll transactions more efficient and paperless.  

[14:0:66,656] The CSPS Project was initiated to modernize the statewide human resource management, travel and business expense, and  

[14:1:66,666] payroll   systems as part of a fully integrated solution. The system should enable the state to improve management processes  

[14:2:66,676] and fulfill its payroll, reporting, and deduction processing obligations accurately and on time.  

[15:0:66,693] 0500400 - UNCLAIMED PROPERTY  

[16:0:66,712] The Unclaimed Property Division administers the Unclaimed Property Law by providing one central source for rightful owners to  

[17:0:66,736] * Dollars in thousands, except in Salary Range. Numbers may not add or match to other statements due to rounding of budget details.

[4:2:66,105] 0500500 - DISBURSEMENTS  

[5:0:66,125] The Disbursements Bureau produces and distributes all payments of the state's obligations after auditing and clearance  

[5:1:66,135] through control accounts.   Examples of some of the state's payments are personal income tax refunds, payroll, vendor  

[5:2:66,144] payments, in-home supportive services and retirement payments. Disbursements also provides post-issuance services,  

[6:0:66,154] including disposition of undeliverable warrants, lost warrant duplication, processing of claims of forgery, and maintenance and  

[6:1:66,164] production of paid warrant images.  

[7:0:66,181] 0500900 - DEPARTMENTAL ADMINISTRATION  

[8:0:66,201] The Executive Office and Administration functions include Budgets, Business Services, Contracts and Procurement,  

[9:0:66,211] Departmental Accounting, and Human Resources all of which provide executive direction and support services to all   divisions  

[9:1:66,220] within SCO.  

[10:0:66,238] The Information Systems Division (ISD)   develops, maintains, and operates all of the department's mainframe, client-server, and  

[10:1:66,247] web-based systems; and oversees the planning, procurement, use, and maintenance of information technology hardware and  

[10:2:66,257] software. Within ISD, the Information Security Office evaluates security compliance, internal control management, and security  

[10:3:66,267] governance.

[9:0:216,450] 0845   Department of Insurance  

[10:0:66,471] California's elected Insurance Commissioner regulates the second largest insurance market in the world with insurance  

[10:1:66,481] companies collecting more than $413 billion in premiums annually in California while protecting consumers and ensuring the  

[10:2:66,491] health and sustainability of the insurance marketplace. The vision of the California Department of Insurance (CDI) is “Insurance  

[10:3:66,501] Protection for All Californians.” CDI accomplishes this by enforcing insurance laws and regulations, assisting consumers in their  

[10:4:66,510] dealings with insurance companies, and enhancing business processes to better address emerging and future challenges as  

[10:5:66,520] well as improving services for insurance producers, consumers, and businesses.  

[11:0:66,537] CDI licenses and regulates insurance companies, and individuals in California. Currently, CDI oversees and licenses more than  

[11:1:66,547] 1,400 insurance companies and more than 495,000 individuals and business entities as insurance agents, brokers, adjusters,  

[11:2:66,557] and bail agents and annually issues approximately 288,000 licenses (new and renewals). Additionally, CDI receives and  

[11:3:66,567] investigates approximately 295,000 consumer inquiries and complaints annually, performs hundreds of ongoing risk-focused  

[11:4:66,576] financial surveillances and market conduct examinations of insurance companies, receives more than 20,000 suspected  

[11:5:66,586] fraudulent claim referrals and investigates approximately 1,300 of those referrals, receives and reviews approximately 5,700  

[11:6:66,596] rate filing applications annually, and works in conjunction with local, state, and federal law enforcement agencies to investigate  

[11:7:66,606] and prosecute fraudulent insurance practices.

[2:0:181,32] 0845   Department of Insurance - Continued 

[3:0:66,68] The objectives of this program are to: (1) oversee the financial solvency of insurance companies to make sure they can provide  

[3:1:66,78] the benefits and protections promised to California policyholders; (2) review complex, principles-based reserving methods and  

[3:2:66,88] underlying assumptions to ensure transparency and consistency in models; (3) prevent unlawful or unfair practices by  

[3:3:66,98] insurance companies and insurance producers as defined by the Insurance Code; (4) ensure that property and casualty  

[3:4:66,108] insurance rates are not excessive, inadequate, unfairly discriminatory, or otherwise in violation of the California Insurance Code  

[3:5:66,117] and the California Code of Regulations; (5) assess and implement strategies to reduce climate risk impacts in the insurance  

[3:6:66,127] sector; (6) review health insurance rates filed with CDI to determine whether they are reasonable and work with insurance  

[3:7:66,137] companies to get unreasonable rates lowered; (7) review long term care insurance rates to ensure requested rate increases  

[3:8:66,147] are actuarially justified and not excessive; (8) perform market conduct reviews to ensure compliance; and (9) ensure that  

[3:9:66,156] applicants for insurance licenses, and holders of insurance licenses, satisfy and maintain the qualifications for licensure.  

[3:10:66,166] Additionally, CDI administers the estates of insolvent and delinquent insurance companies through the Conservation and  

[3:11:66,176] Liquidation Office.  

[3:12:66,193] 0525 - CONSUMER PROTECTION  

[4:0:66,211] The objectives of this program are to: (1) provide direct service to California consumers by protecting insurance policyholders  

[4:1:66,220] and other parties involved in insurance transactions against unfair or illegal practices with respect to claims handling, rating, or  

[4:2:66,230] underwriting by insurers and producers; (2) protect applicants and policyholders from discriminatory, unlawful, or fraudulent  

[4:3:66,240] practices and from incompetence relating to the sale of insurance; (3) oversee programs that benefit California's underserved  

[4:4:66,250] and historically disadvantaged communities; and (4) assist wildfire survivors, local governments, small businesses, community  

[4:5:66,259] service organizations, and neighborhood associations by providing access to CDI’s services through electronic and printed  

[4:6:66,269] informational guides, workshops, seminars, roundtables, and town halls.  

[4:7:66,286] 0530 - FRAUD CONTROL  

[5:0:66,304] The objective of this program is to protect the public from economic loss by actively investigating, arresting and referring for  

[5:1:66,314] prosecution those who commit insurance fraud and other violations of the law. The program is primarily staffed by sworn peace  

[5:2:66,323] officers who conduct criminal investigations of insurance fraud and related criminal cases. The program administers the  

[5:3:66,333] Insurance Frauds Prevention Act (IFPA), which authorizes CDI to conduct criminal insurance fraud investigations. In addition,  

[5:4:66,343] the IFPA authorizes the Commissioner to initiate or intervene in whistle-blower “qui tam” actions seeking civil penalties against  

[5:5:66,353] perpetrators of insurance fraud.  

[6:0:66,370] 0535 - GENERAL FUND TAX COLLECTION AND COMPLIANCE  

[7:0:66,387] This program performs tax collection, ensures the compliance of insurance companies and surplus line brokers with the laws  

[7:1:66,397] contained in the Insurance Code and the Revenue and Taxation Code, and works with the California Department of Tax and  

[7:2:66,407] Fee Administration and the State Controller's Office on various refund, assessment, and accounting matters relative to the  

[7:3:66,416] premium tax program. Tax collections from this program are deposited in the state's General Fund.

[8:0:183,343] 0850   California State Lottery Commission  

[9:0:66,364] In 1984, Proposition 37 amended the California Constitution to authorize the establishment of a statewide lottery. As an  

[9:1:66,374] initiative statute, the California State Lottery Act (Act) of 1984 created the California State Lottery Commission and gave it  

[9:2:66,383] broad powers to oversee the operations of a statewide lottery. The purpose of the Act was to provide supplemental monies to  

[9:3:66,393] benefit public education. The Lottery is overseen by a five-person Commission appointed by the Governor and confirmed by  

[9:4:66,403] the State Senate.  

[10:0:66,420] The Act initially required that 50 percent of total annual revenues be returned to the public in the form of prizes and at least 34  

[10:1:66,430] percent of total revenues be allocated to the benefit of public education. No more than 16 percent of total revenues were to be  

[10:2:66,440] used for administrative costs.  

[11:0:66,457] In 2010, the Act was changed to allow the Lottery flexibility to pay out more money in prizes and reduce the administrative cost  

[11:1:66,467] limit to 13 percent of total revenues. Along with that flexibility, the new law requires the Lottery to meet minimum levels of  

[11:2:66,476] contribution to public education. Revenues to education are placed in a special fund, known as the California State Lottery  

[11:3:66,486] Education Fund, which holds revenues until they are allocated on a per capita basis, using prior year certified Average Daily  

[11:4:66,496] Attendance data, to the following categories: K-12 education, Community Colleges, the California State University, the  

[11:5:66,506] University of California, and other educational entities, including the California Schools for the Deaf and Blind.  

[12:0:66,523] In the 38 years since sales began in October 1985 through June 30, 2023, the California State Lottery has raised $43.8 billion  

[12:1:66,533] for public education, including approximately $2.3 billion in 2022-23.  

[13:0:66,550] Because of the inherently variable nature of lottery ticket sales, revenue estimates for 2023-24 and 2024-25 cannot be made  

[13:1:66,560] with certainty.  

[14:0:66,586] LEGAL CITATIONS AND AUTHORITY  

[14:1:66,605] DEPARTMENT AUTHORITY  

[15:0:66,625] Government Code, Title 2, Division 1, Chapter 12.5 (Sections 8880-8880.72).  

[16:0:66,736] * Dollars in thousands, except in Salary Range. Numbers may not add or match to other statements due to rounding of budget details.

[1:0:169,65] 0855   California Gambling Control Commission  

[2:0:66,86] The mission of the California Gambling Control Commission (Commission) is to protect the public by ensuring integrity and  

[2:1:66,96] justice in California's controlled gambling industry through effective regulations and fair application of the law. The Commission  

[2:2:66,106] has jurisdiction over gambling establishments (cardrooms), third-party providers of proposition player services, and certain  

[2:3:66,115] aspects of Tribal casinos, pursuant to its authority under state law and Tribal-State Gaming Compacts (Compacts). The  

[2:4:66,125] Commission also has jurisdiction over gaming policies, regulations, criteria, and standards.  

[3:0:66,142] There are 81 licensed cardrooms in California over which the Commission has broad regulatory and adjudicatory authority. This  

[3:1:66,152] authority extends to the operation, concentration, and supervision of the cardrooms and all persons and things related to each  

[3:2:66,162] licensed establishment.  

[4:0:66,179] The Commission has fiduciary, regulatory, and administrative responsibilities related to Tribal gaming that include: (1)  

[4:1:66,189] distributing specified Tribal gaming revenues to Non-Compact, Non-Gaming and Limited-Gaming Tribes; (2) making suitability  

[4:2:66,199] determinations for key employees, gaming resource suppliers, and financial sources; and (3) serving as the administrator of the  

[4:3:66,209] Indian Gaming Revenue Sharing Trust Fund, Indian Gaming Special Distribution Fund, and the Tribal Nation Grant Fund.

[3:0:66,68] This program   values and assesses   inter-county pipelines and properties owned or used by railroads, regulated telephone

[3:3:66,98] that is deposited in the state General Fund.  

[3:4:66,115] 0570150 - ALCOHOLIC BEVERAGE TAX PROGRAM  

[4:0:66,135] This program collects the excise tax imposed on the sale, distribution, or importation of alcoholic beverages in California. The  

[4:1:66,144] BOE contracts with the CDTFA to administer this program.  

[4:2:66,162] 0570575 - INSURANCE TAX PROGRAM  

[5:0:66,181] This program provides revenue to the Insurance Tax Fund. Revenue is generated from taxes assessed on insurance  

[5:1:66,191] premiums, underwriting profits from ocean marine insurance, and retaliatory assessments levied on   out-of-state insurers. The  

[5:2:66,201] taxes collected are used to pay refunds or transferred to the General Fund. The BOE contracts with the CDTFA to administer  

[5:3:66,211] this program.

[6:0:236,220] 0890   Secretary of State  

[7:0:66,241] The Secretary of State, a constitutionally established office, is the chief elections officer of the state and is responsible for the  

[7:1:66,251] administration and enforcement of election laws. The Office also is responsible for administering and enforcing laws pertaining  

[7:2:66,261] to filing documents associated with corporations, limited liability companies, partnerships, limited partnerships, unincorporated  

[7:3:66,271] associations and pertaining to filing bonds and perfecting security agreements. In addition, the Office is responsible for  

[7:4:66,281] commissioning notaries public, enforcing the notary laws, and in conjunction with being the home of the State Archives,  

[8:0:66,290] administering a state records management program and preserving documents and records having historical significance. The  

[8:1:66,300] Secretary of State is the filing officer for lobbying and campaign registration and disclosure documents filed under the Political  

[8:2:66,310] Reform Act. The Secretary of State also operates the Safe at Home program, maintains the Domestic Partners and Advance  

[8:3:66,320] Health Care Directives registries, provides support functions for the Voting Modernization Board, and is home to the California  

[8:4:66,329] Museum.  

[9:0:66,349] Policy associated with the administration of the Office is accomplished through the divisions of Elections, Political Reform,  

[9:1:66,359] Business Programs, Archives, Information Technology, and Management Services.

[5:0:66,214] PROGRAM DESCRIPTIONS  

[6:0:66,232] 0700 - FILINGS AND REGISTRATIONS  

[7:0:66,252] The Business Programs Division is comprised of three primary areas: Business Entities, Uniform Commercial Code, and  

[7:1:66,262] Notary Public and Special Filings.  

[8:0:66,281] Business Entities examines, files, and maintains formation documents for new California business entities and qualifies out-of-

[8:1:66,291] state and international entities to conduct business in California; and also examines, files and maintains changes to the records  

[8:2:66,301] of domestic or qualified foreign business entities to ensure the business entities are properly formed, merged, converted,  

[8:3:66,311] amended, and dissolved in compliance with California law. Statements of Information containing key officers and contact  

[8:4:66,320] information are also processed and maintained for public and governmental use.  

[9:0:66,340] Uniform Commercial Code files certain financing statements allowing lending institutions to protect their financial interest in  

[9:1:66,350] personal property and established priority in case of debtor default or bankruptcy. Notices of judgment liens, attachment liens,  

[9:2:66,359] agricultural liens, and equipment liens are also filed in Uniform Commercial Code, as well as notices of various tax liens  

[9:3:66,369] submitted by state and federal taxing agencies such as the Franchise Tax Board and the Internal Revenue Service.  

[10:0:66,389] Notary Public and Special Filings appoints and commissions qualified notaries public for four- year terms, reviews and  

[10:1:66,399] approves notary public education courses, and certifies to the authenticity of signatures of notaries public and public officials on  

[10:2:66,408] documents for use in foreign countries. Special Filings include trademarks and service marks as well as surety bonds that  

[10:3:66,418] certain business entities are required to file, notices of joint power agreements, city and county charters, and claims for  

[10:4:66,428] successor-in-interest. This area also has disciplinary functions with regard to notaries public and immigration consultants.  

[11:0:66,447] The Advance Health Care Directive (AHCD) Registry maintains AHCDs, which allow a person to indicate his/her medical  

[11:1:66,457] treatment preferences if he/she cannot speak or make decisions for him/herself. The AHCD also may designate someone else  

[11:2:66,467] to make decisions regarding medical treatment. AHCDs and related information can be provided upon request to the  

[11:3:66,477] registrant's health care provider, public guardian, or legal representative.  

[12:0:66,496] The Domestic Partners Registry registers same-sex couples regardless of the age of the partners, and opposite-sex couples in  

[12:1:66,506] which one partner is at least 62 years old, as domestic partners in California.  

[13:0:66,526] The Victims of Corporate Fraud Compensation Fund provides limited restitution to victims of corporate fraud who are unable to  

[13:1:66,535] collect through other means on a judgment against a corporation for corporate fraud.  

[14:0:66,553] Safe at Home is the State's confidential address program designed to assist: (1) victims and survivors of domestic violence and  

[14:1:66,562] human trafficking, sexual assault, stalking, and elder/dependent adult abuse; (2) reproductive health care employees, patients,  

[14:2:66,572] and volunteers who are in fear for their safety because of their affiliation with a health care facility; (3) public entity employees  

[14:3:66,582] and contractors who are in fear for their safety because of their work for a public entity; and (4) their household members and  

[14:4:66,592] minor children. Participants in the Safe at Home program are provided with a post office box to use as a substitute mailing  

[14:5:66,602] address, thereby allowing them to keep their actual residential address confidential. Once enrolled into the program, Safe at  

[14:6:66,611] Home   provides a mail forwarding service and assists participants who qualify with additional confidential services offered by  

[14:7:66,621] various state and local agencies.  

[15:0:66,638] 0705 - ELECTIONS  

[16:0:66,658] The Secretary of State serves as California’s chief elections officer and has broad responsibility to administer and oversee  

[16:1:66,668] federal and state elections held within California. The Elections Program maintains the official statewide database of registered  

[16:2:66,677] voters, tracks and certifies ballot measures, certifies candidates for placement on the ballot, and certifies official election  

[16:3:66,687] results. In addition, the program oversees the certification and modernization of voting equipment and the enhancement of  

[16:4:66,697] election processes. The program works to increase accessibility to voters, provides resources to educate voters on the  

[16:5:66,707] electoral system, and investigates election related criminal violations as provided by the California Elections Code.

[3:0:66,78] This program includes the campaign and lobbying registration and disclosure provisions of the Political Reform Act of 1974.  

[3:1:66,88] Under this law, the Office registers all levels of campaign committees, and reviews for compliance with statutory requirements  

[3:2:66,98] all state-level candidate and campaign recipient committees, major donors, slate mailer, political party, and independent  

[3:3:66,108] expenditure committees. The program registers lobbyists (including placement agents), lobbying firms and lobbyist employers,  

[3:4:66,117] reviews for compliance with statutory requirements the periodic financial disclosure reports filed by lobbying entities, and  

[3:5:66,127] publishes a Lobbying Directory on the Internet. This program also supports the electronic filing requirements of the Political  

[3:6:66,137] Reform Act and posts relevant registration and disclosure information to the Office's web site so the public can track where  

[3:7:66,147] campaign money is raised and spent and what lobbying activity is occurring in state government. A searchable database of  

[3:8:66,156] campaign and lobbying financial activity is also provided through the web site.  

[3:9:66,174] 0710 - ARCHIVES  

[4:0:66,193] The California State Archives establishes and administers a records management program that applies efficient and  

[4:1:66,203] economical management methods to the creation, utilization, maintenance, retention, preservation, and disposal of state  

[4:2:66,213] records and acquires, indexes, preserves, and provides reference access to irreplaceable historic materials from the three  

[4:3:66,223] branches of state government. The Archives acquires physical and electronic records with legal, administrative, or evidentiary  

[4:4:66,232] value, or that have historical significance. Materials are transferred to the Archives for permanent preservation.  

[4:5:66,250] 9900 - ADMINISTRATION  

[5:0:66,269] This program develops and manages overall policy. It serves as the liaison to other state agencies, the Legislature, the federal  

[5:1:66,279] government, and other states' Secretaries of State. This program responds to constituent correspondence and requests from  

[5:2:66,289] the public and advocates in the Legislature, administers the Secretary's role as a member of the National Association of  

[5:3:66,299] Secretaries of State, in the management of task forces and advisory committees, in the implementation of new federal and  

[5:4:66,308] state statutory requirements, and coordinates and disseminates information about the Office and its initiatives to the public.  

[5:5:66,318] This program also monitors elections cybersecurity incidents and promotes transparency in alerting the public regarding false  

[5:6:66,328] election websites and information as well as providing oversight on cybersecurity risk management.   It also provides the Office  

[5:7:66,338] with the necessary personnel, general administrative, budgeting, fiscal services, and information technology expertise to plan,  

[5:8:66,347] develop, implement, and operate innovative and effective business and information systems solutions.

[5:0:243,140] 0950   State Treasurer  

[6:0:66,161] The State Treasurer, a constitutionally established office, provides banking services for state government with goals to  

[6:1:66,171] minimize interest and service costs and to maximize yield on investments. The Treasurer is responsible for the custody of all  

[6:2:66,180] monies and securities belonging to or held in trust by the state; investment of temporarily idle state monies; administration of  

[6:3:66,190] the sale of state bonds, their redemption and interest payments; and payment of warrants or checks drawn by the State  

[6:4:66,200] Controller and other state agencies.

[5:0:66,151] PROGRAM DESCRIPTIONS  

[6:0:66,170] 0740010 - INVESTMENT SERVICES  

[7:0:66,190] The Investment Division is responsible for investment of state monies from the date of receipt through the date of redemption.  

[7:1:66,199] During the 2022-23 fiscal year, this Division handled 11,347 security investment transactions totaling $546.5 billion. The Pooled  

[7:2:66,209] Money Investment Board (PMIB) program accounted for 10,197 of these transactions totaling $507.5 billion; time deposits  

[7:3:66,219] accounted for 848 transactions totaling $27.6 billion. The remaining $11 billion is invested on behalf of the state's special funds,  

[7:4:66,229] such as those associated with the California Housing Finance Agency, the Department of Fish and Wildlife, the state's  

[7:5:66,238] retirement system, etc. The Division also administers the Local Agency Investment Fund (LAIF), a voluntary investment  

[7:6:66,248] program created to offer California local agencies greater access to the financial markets through the PMIB program. In the  

[7:7:66,258] 2022-23 fiscal year, 2,361 local agencies participated in LAIF, with deposits averaging $28.7 billion for the fiscal year.  

[8:0:66,275] 0740019 - CENTRALIZED TREASURY AND SECURITIES MANAGEMENT  

[9:0:66,295] In 1949, the California Legislature amended Government Code Section 16305 to create the Centralized Treasury System  

[9:1:66,305] (CTS) thereby requiring agencies of the State to deposit their money in trust with the Treasurer. The legislation also requires  

[9:2:66,314] the Treasurer to safeguard the money and make safe and prudent investments. In 1955, the Legislature created the PMIB,  

[9:3:66,324] giving the PMIB the responsibility to designate the amount of money available for the investment in securities, bank deposits,  

[9:4:66,334] and loans to the General Fund.  

[10:0:66,354] The Centralized Treasury and Securities Management Division (CTSMD) oversees all banking aspects of the CTS. The goal of  

[10:1:66,363] the CTSMD is to maximize the earning of interest consistent with safe and prudent treasury management, and to ensure that  

[10:2:66,373] the depository banks provide the state with proper and adequate security for the deposit of state monies. The Treasurer  

[10:3:66,383] maintains demand bank accounts with seven banks for the purpose of providing necessary statewide depository coverage for  

[10:4:66,393] the remittance of funds collected by the various state agencies.  

[11:0:66,412] The CTSMD 1) monitors the cash flow of all state funds, forecasts cash balances, revenue, expenditures, and the amounts  

[11:1:66,422] available for daily investments, 2) ensures accurate and timely agency deposits, 3) administers and executes the wire transfer  

[11:2:66,432] of funds, 4) reconciles state accounts with depository banks, 5) redeems all state items submitted by presenting banks for  

[11:3:66,441] payment, 6) executes the clearance and income collection for state investments (excluding PERS and STRS) and securities  

[11:4:66,451] pledged to the state, 7) fulfills the clearance and settlement of securities pledged to the state as collateral for the time, demand,  

[11:5:66,461] and other state agency programs, and 8) is responsible for the safekeeping of securities and other personal property owned by  

[11:6:66,471] or pledged to the state.  

[12:0:66,488] 0740028 - PUBLIC FINANCE  

[13:0:66,508] The Public Finance Division is responsible for selling State of California general obligation bonds, commercial paper notes,  

[13:1:66,517] revenue anticipation notes, lease revenue bonds, revenue bonds, and any other indebtedness for all state agencies. The  

[13:2:66,527] Division provides debt administration services for most state bonds and is responsible for disseminating information to  

[13:3:66,537] bondholders through the Investor Relations Program.  

[14:0:66,556] The Division also assures compliance with federal tax laws applicable to certain state debt so there is no adverse  

[14:1:66,566] consequences to bondholders or increased borrowing costs to the state. The post-issuance tax compliance   includes monitoring  

[14:2:66,576] the use of bond proceeds, managing rebate and yield restriction compliance, coordinating necessary financial disclosure, and  

[14:3:66,586] ensuring proper record keeping.  

[15:0:66,603] 0740035 - ADMINISTRATION AND INFORMATION SERVICES  

[16:0:66,620] The Administration Division, Executive Office, and the Information Services Division provide executive direction and support  

[16:1:66,630] services to programs in the State Treasurer's Office. Services include budgeting, personnel, accounting, information systems,  

[16:2:66,640] business services, technical support, and production operations.  

[17:0:66,736] * Dollars in thousands, except in Salary Range. Numbers may not add or match to other statements due to rounding of budget details.

[8:1:66,360] 0800 - CALIFORNIA DEBT AND INVESTMENT ADVISORY COMMISSION  

[9:0:66,380] The Commission's primary objective is to enhance the knowledge of public officials with respect to the administration and  

[9:1:66,390] issuance of public debt and the investment of public funds. The Commission achieves this objective by performing functions in  

[9:2:66,399] three principal areas, including:  

[10:0:65,417] • Data Collection and Analysis - Serves as a clearinghouse of debt issued by California public entities and nonprofit student  

[10:1:76,426] loan corporations.  

[11:0:65,442] • Continuing Education - Provides educational seminars, workshops, and conferences to public officials on municipal debt and  

[11:1:76,452] public fund investment topics. In addition, the Commission provides technical assistance to public officials and taxpayers  

[11:2:76,462] through direct interaction and public forums to disseminate relevant information.  

[11:3:65,478] • Policy Research - Undertakes original research on the issuance and administration of public debt and on the investment of  

[11:4:76,487] public funds. Research results are published in the form of reports, issue briefs, or articles and are disseminated in printed  

[11:5:76,497] and electronic forms.

[0:0:66,13] LEGISLATIVE, JUDICIAL, AND EXECUTIVE   2024-25 STATE BUDGET — LJE   215 

[1:0:165,69] 0959   California Debt Limit Allocation Committee  

[2:0:66,90] The California Debt Limit Allocation Committee's mission is to allocate tax-exempt, private activity bond authority for the State  

[2:1:66,100] of California. Private activity bonds may be used by the private sector for projects and programs that provide a public benefit,  

[2:2:66,109] such as the creation of affordable housing.  

[3:0:66,127] The federal government limits the total amount of tax-exempt private activity bond authority that can be issued on an annual  

[3:1:66,136] basis. The bond authority limit (debt limit) is calculated by multiplying the state population by $120 in 2023; a factor determined  

[3:2:66,146] by the IRS each year. California's limit totaled almost $4.7 billion in 2023.  

[4:0:66,163] The Committee's allocation of tax-exempt bond authority results in the issuance of bonds by cities, counties, joint powers  

[4:1:66,173] authorities and state agencies. The bonds are purchased and used by the private sector and are not an obligation of the state  

[4:2:66,183] or federal government.  

[5:0:66,200] The Committee is funded on a fee-supported basis and is comprised of six members, including three voting members and three  

[5:1:66,210] advisors. The voting members are the State Treasurer (Chairperson of the Committee), the Governor, and the State Controller  

[5:2:66,220] or their designee. The non-voting (advisory) members are the Director of Housing and Community Development, the Executive  

[5:3:66,230] Director of the California Housing Finance Agency, and a representative from local government selected by two voting  

[5:4:66,239] members.  

[6:0:66,269] 3-YEAR EXPENDITURES AND POSITIONS  

[7:0:340,291] Positions Expenditures  

[7:1:305,304] 2022-23 2023-24 2024-25 2022-23* 2023-24* 2024-25*

[14:0:66,701] The Committee administers various programs that are funded through the allocation and issuance of tax-exempt private activity

[1:0:382,13] LEGISLATIVE, JUDICIAL, AND EXECUTIVE 

[2:0:130,32] 0959   California Debt Limit Allocation Committee - Continued 

[3:0:66,68] Qualified Residential Rental Project Program - Tax-exempt housing revenue bonds lower the cost to develop multifamily rental  

[3:1:66,78] housing, to acquire land and construct new housing, or purchase and rehabilitate existing housing. The developers produce  

[3:2:66,88] affordable rental housing for low-income households by reducing rental rates charged to these individuals and families. To  

[3:3:66,98] qualify, developers must elect either to reserve at least 20% of the units for households earning up to 50% AMI or reserve at  

[3:4:66,108] least 40% of the units for households earning up to 60% AMI. Projects that receive an award of bond authority can apply for  

[3:5:66,117] four-percent tax credits using the California Debt Limit Allocation Committee/California Tax Credit Allocation Committee Joint  

[3:6:66,127] Application.  

[4:0:66,144] Exempt Facility Program - Tax-exempt private activity bonds issued to finance any of the following facilities identified by the IRS  

[4:1:66,154] as an exempt facility, including but not limited to: solid waste disposal and waste recycling facilities, mass commuting facilities,  

[4:2:66,164] high-speed rail, energy and power generating facilities, and sustainable design facilities. Loan interest rate savings enable the  

[4:3:66,174] project owners to charge lower customer rates, while assisting communities they serve to meet mandated requirements to  

[4:4:66,183] protect and enhance the environment.  

[5:0:66,201] Single-Family Housing Program - Tax-exempt mortgage revenue bonds or mortgage credit certificates to assist first-time  

[5:1:66,211] homebuyers with purchasing homes. Homebuyers may purchase single-family homes, either freestanding or detached,  

[5:2:66,220] condominiums, or townhouses. Program participants must meet income limits and must purchase a home that falls within the  

[5:3:66,230] program's purchase price limitations.  

[6:0:66,247] Industrial Development Bond Project Program - Small-Issue industrial development bonds are tax-exempt private activity bonds  

[6:1:66,257] issued to assist manufacturing facilities finance capital expenditures. These bonds offer interest rate savings to small and  

[6:2:66,267] midsize manufacturers in contrast to conventional loans, and help manufacturers retain and create new jobs within their  

[6:3:66,277] communities.

[14:0:166,660] 0968   California Tax Credit Allocation Committee  

[15:0:66,681] The mission of the California Tax Credit Allocation Committee is to fairly allocate federal and state tax credits to create and  

[15:1:66,690] maintain safe, quality affordable rental housing for low-income households in California.  

[16:0:66,736] * Dollars in thousands, except in Salary Range. Numbers may not add or match to other statements due to rounding of budget details.

[2:0:131,32] 0968   California Tax Credit Allocation Committee - Continued 

[3:0:66,68] The Committee assists development of rental housing projects for low-income families and monitors these projects for years  

[3:1:66,78] after they are placed in service to ensure compliance with federal and state tax credit program requirements of the Internal  

[3:2:66,88] Revenue Service, State law and the Committee. Coordinating with state and local housing fund providers, and with private  

[3:3:66,98] investors, the Committee helps to create and maintain a supply of quality, affordable housing throughout California.  

[3:4:66,115] The Committee consists of seven members, including five voting members and two advisors. The voting members are the  

[3:5:66,125] State Treasurer (Chairperson of the Committee), the Governor, the State Controller, the Director of Housing and Community  

[3:6:66,135] Development, and the Executive Director of the California Housing Finance Agency or their designee. The non-voting  

[3:7:66,144] (advisory) members are two local government representatives. One local representative is associated with a city government  

[3:8:66,154] and appointed by the Speaker of the Assembly. The other is a county government representative appointed by the Senate  

[3:9:66,164] Rules Committee.

[11:0:66,633] PROGRAM DESCRIPTIONS  

[12:0:66,652] 0840 - CALIFORNIA TAX CREDIT ALLOCATION COMMITTEE  

[13:0:66,672] The Federal Program  

[14:0:66,689] Congress authorized the federal Low-Income Housing Tax Credit (LIHTC) program in 1986. It replaced traditional housing tax  

[14:1:66,699] incentives, such as accelerated depreciation, with a tax credit that enables developers of affordable rental housing to raise  

[15:0:66,736] * Dollars in thousands, except in Salary Range. Numbers may not add or match to other statements due to rounding of budget details.

# === PAGE 221 === [size: 612x792]
[0:0:66,13] LEGISLATIVE, JUDICIAL, AND EXECUTIVE 

[1:0:410,13] 2024-25 STATE BUDGET — LJE   219 

[2:0:131,32] 0968   California Tax Credit Allocation Committee - Continued 

[3:0:66,68] project equity through the "sale" of tax benefits to investors. The federal per-capita tax credit ceiling is calculated annually and  

[3:1:66,78] indexed for inflation.  

[4:0:66,96] Two types of federal tax credits are available, generally referred to as 9-percent and 4-percent credits. These terms refer to the  

[4:1:66,105] percentage of a project's "qualified basis" an investor in LIHTC projects may deduct from his or her federal income tax liability  

[4:2:66,115] annually in each of ten years. A project's qualified basis is the portion of the total project cost used to calculate the amount of  

[4:3:66,125] the tax credit and typically excludes non-depreciable costs such as the purchase of land. The affordable housing project  

[4:4:66,135] sponsor uses or sells ten times the project's tax-credit allocation amount because investors can take the annual tax credit each  

[4:5:66,144] year for a ten-year period.  

[5:0:66,162] The federal per-capita tax credit ceiling for 9-percent credits is calculated annually and indexed for inflation. For 2023, each  

[5:1:66,171] state has an annual housing credit ceiling of $2.75 per state resident. Additionally, each state may qualify annually for a share  

[5:2:66,181] of unused credits in a national pool.  

[6:0:66,198] Developments financed with tax-exempt bond proceeds may receive the 4% federal tax credit. The sponsors of such projects  

[6:1:66,208] must apply to the Committee and must conform to applicable federal and state statutory and regulatory requirements, but there  

[6:2:66,218] is no annual cap on the amount of credit that may be awarded by the state to such developments.  

[6:3:66,235] Under federal law, credit projects must remain affordable for at least 30 years. However, California's program generally requires  

[6:4:66,245] maintaining affordability for 55 years. Land use agreements are recorded against each credit project to ensure compliance.  

[6:5:66,262] The State LIHTC Program  

[7:0:66,280] In 1987, recognizing the high cost of housing in California, the Legislature authorized the state's program to augment the  

[7:1:66,289] federal program. Housing projects receiving (or that have previously received) an allocation of federal tax credits may apply for  

[7:2:66,299] an allocation of state tax credits. Projects serving farmworkers may apply for state tax credits regardless of their federal tax  

[7:3:66,309] credit status.  

[8:0:66,326] The annual state credit ceiling is indexed for inflation, and for 2023, was $118.6 million. Investors take the state tax credit  

[8:1:66,336] annually over a four-year period in contrast to the ten-year federal period.  

[8:2:66,353] Chapter 159, Statutes of 2019, authorized the California Tax Credit Allocation Committee and the California Debt Limit  

[8:3:66,363] Allocation Committee to issue an additional $500 million in state low-income housing tax credits in 2020 to be paired with 4%  

[8:4:66,373] federal low-income housing tax credits and tax-exempt bonds to bolster new construction of low-income housing. Per AB 101,  

[8:5:66,383] this $500 million tax credit was issued for calendar years beginning in 2020, and for calendar years beginning in 2021 pursuant  

[8:6:66,392] to an authorization in the annual Budget Act or related legislation.  

[8:7:66,410] Farmworker Housing Program  

[9:0:66,427] Chapter 521, Statutes of 2008, folded the previous Farmworker Housing Assistance Tax Credit program into the general state  

[9:1:66,437] LIHTC program as a $500,000 annual set-aside in support of farmworker housing. Pursuant to Chapter 372, Statutes of 2017,  

[9:2:66,446] these credits differ from other state credits in that the credit percentage for tax-exempt bond financed projects is higher and  

[9:3:66,456] projects may receive state credits even if they receive a federal tax credit basis increase.  

[9:4:66,474] Beginning in 2024 through 2034 calendar year, AB 1654 allocates the lesser of 5% or $25,000,000 of the $500,000,000 state  

[9:5:66,483] low-income housing tax credits to be set aside for farmworker housing.  

[9:6:66,501] State Historic Rehabilitation Tax Credits  

[10:0:66,518] SB 451 authorized $50,000,000 in historic tax credits per calendar year, for a five-year period, beginning in 2021. These tax  

[10:1:66,528] credits are allocated by California Tax Credit Allocation Committee in conjunction with and upon approval from the Office of  

[10:2:66,537] Historic Preservation.

[7:0:88,320] 0971   California Alternative Energy and Advanced Transportation Financing  

[7:1:279,334] Authority  

[8:0:66,355] The California Alternative Energy and Advanced Transportation Financing Authority was established by Chapter 908, Statutes  

[8:1:66,365] of 1980, to promote the prompt and efficient development of energy sources which are renewable or which more efficiently  

[8:2:66,375] utilize and conserve scarce energy resources. Recognizing the importance of developing a secure energy future to protect the  

[8:3:66,384] environment and ensure economic stability, the Authority advances the state's goals of reducing greenhouse gas emissions by  

[8:4:66,394] providing financing options to assist industries in increasing the deployment of sustainable and renewable energy sources,  

[8:5:66,404] implementing measures that increase the efficiency of the use of energy, creating high quality employment opportunities, and  

[8:6:66,414] lessening the state's dependence on fossil fuels. To support these goals, the Authority provides financing and credit  

[9:0:66,423] enhancements to promote the establishment of facilities that use alternative methods and sources of energy, and facilities  

[9:1:66,433] needed for the development and commercialization of advanced transportation technologies.  

[10:0:66,453] The Authority consists of five members: the State Treasurer, who serves as the chairperson, the State Controller, the Director  

[10:1:66,463] of the Department of Finance, the Chairperson of the State Energy Resources Conservation and Development Commission,  

[10:2:66,472] and the President of the Public Utilities Commission.

[7:0:66,267] PROGRAM DESCRIPTIONS  

[8:0:66,286] 0850 - ALTERNATIVE ENERGY AND ADVANCED TRANSPORTATION FINANCING AUTHORITY  

[9:0:66,305] Financial assistance can take the form of loans, loan loss reserves, interest rate reductions, proceeds of bonds issued by the  

[9:1:66,315] Authority,   loan guarantees or other credit enhancements, contributions of money, or a combination thereof, as determined and  

[9:2:66,325] approved by resolution of the Authority.  

[10:0:66,342] The Authority is a conduit issuer of tax-exempt private activity bonds to finance local district heating or cooling facilities, and  

[10:1:66,352] private activity bonds for 501(c)(3) non-profits. Past issuances total more than $212 million in bond financing for green projects  

[10:2:66,362] in California.  

[11:0:66,381] In 2010, the Authority launched a sales and use tax exclusion (STE) program for advanced transportation and alternative  

[11:1:66,391] source manufacturing in California (Chapter 10, Statutes of 2010). The STE program   expanded   (Chapter 677, Statutes of  

[11:2:66,401] 2012)   to include advanced manufacturing, with an annual cap of $100 million available for STE awards. Chapter 768, Statutes  

[11:3:66,410] of 2015, further expanded the program to also include equipment primarily used to process recycled feedstock and equipment  

[11:4:66,420] that primarily utilizes recycled feedstock in the production of another product.   In 2019, Chapter 690, Statutes of 2019, extended  

[11:5:66,430] the STE program sunset date to January 1, 2026, and Chapter 672, Statutes of 2019, expanded the job creation-related criteria  

[11:6:66,440] by which CAEATFA must evaluate applications. In 2022, Chapter 251, Statutes of 2022, authorized CAEATFA an additional  

[11:7:66,450] $15 million per calendar year in STE for specific lithium projects for years 2022 to 2024. The objective of this program is to  

[11:8:66,459] promote the creation of California-based manufacturing, businesses, and jobs that are aimed at reducing greenhouse gases,  

[11:9:66,469] air and water pollution, and energy consumption.  

[12:0:66,489] Legislation in 2010 (Chapter 15, Statutes of 2010), 2011 (Chapter 9, Statutes of 2011, First Extraordinary Session), and 2013  

[12:1:66,498] (Chapter 356, Statutes of 2013), authorized financial assistance to encourage energy efficiency retrofits. The Authority  

[12:2:66,508] developed a loan loss reserve program   for residential whole house energy efficiency and renewable energy projects, and   a risk  

[12:3:66,518] mitigation program for residential Property Assessed Clean Energy (PACE) programs in California. A program to support low-

[12:4:66,528] interest financing of PACE efficiency projects in commercial buildings is in discussion.  

[13:0:66,547] The Budget Act of 2014 authorized administration of the California Hub for Energy Efficiency Financing on behalf of, and in  

[13:1:66,557] collaboration with, the California Public Utilities Commission   to develop and launch energy efficiency financing pilot programs   to  

[13:2:66,567] encourage and leverage private capital lending   (in both the residential and commercial sectors). These programs   employ  

[13:3:66,577] various types of credit enhancements, lower costs and expand access to capital for Californians making   energy efficiency  

[13:4:66,586] improvements   to help achieve the state's   energy efficiency and decarbonization   goals.   As current funding sources limit  

[13:5:66,596] eligibility to customers of the four Investor Owned Utilities, additional funding sources to expand statewide access to energy  

[13:6:66,606] efficiency improvements are under consideration.

[6:0:151,199] 0974   California Pollution Control Financing Authority  

[7:0:66,220] California Pollution Control Financing Authority (CPCFA) is committed to stimulating local economies in California by making  

[7:1:66,230] capital more accessible to small businesses and delivering innovative financing for projects that protect and restore the  

[7:2:66,240] environment.  

[8:0:66,257] Created in 1972, CPCFA originated as a conduit issuer of tax-exempt, pollution-control bonds and now assists the solid-waste,  

[8:1:66,267] recycling, water, and wastewater industries through its Private Activity Tax-Exempt Bond Program. Over the last 50 years,  

[8:2:66,277] CPCFA evolved to include financing programs assisting California's small business sector through its California Capital Access  

[8:3:66,287] Program (CalCAP), the California Investment and Innovation Program (Cal IIP), and to promote the reuse and redevelopment  

[8:4:66,296] of brownfields through the California Recycle Underutilized Sites Program (CALReUSE).  

[9:0:66,316] CPCFA consists of the following members: State Treasurer (Chairperson), the State Controller, and the Director of the  

[9:1:66,326] Department of Finance.  

[10:0:66,355] 3-YEAR EXPENDITURES AND POSITIONS

[5:0:66,189] PROGRAM DESCRIPTIONS  

[6:0:66,208] 0860 - POLLUTION CONTROL TAX-EXEMPT BOND PROGRAM  

[7:0:66,225] CPCFA’s Tax-Exempt Bond Program provides access to the tax-exempt bond market for private companies serving a public  

[7:1:66,235] purpose, through the use of private activity bonds. California businesses may use private activity bonds for the acquisition,  

[7:2:66,245] construction, or installation of qualified waste disposal, waste recovery, water furnishing, and wastewater treatment, or the  

[7:3:66,254] acquisition and installation of new equipment. In addition, CPCFA maintains a Small Business Assistance Fund (SBAF) that  

[7:4:66,264] provides cost of issuance financial assistance to qualified small business borrowers.  

[8:0:66,281] Tax-exempt financing issued by CPCFA assists municipalities in complying with waste diversion mandates of the California  

[8:1:66,291] Integrated Waste Management Act of 1989, the California Global Warming Solutions Act of 2006, and more recently, Assembly  

[8:2:66,301] Bill 1826 (Chesbro, Chapter 727, Statutes of 2014), and Senate Bill 1383 (Lara, Chapter 395, Statutes of 2016). From 1974  

[8:3:66,311] through June 30, 2023, CPCFA issued bonds totaling approximately $17.1 billion.  

[9:0:66,328] 0865 - CALIFORNIA CAPITAL ACCESS PROGRAM FOR SMALL BUSINESSES  

[10:0:66,345] CalCAP for Small Business administers a loan loss reserve program that incentivizes participating financial institutions to make  

[10:1:66,355] loans to small businesses. The program works through the creation of a loan loss-reserve fund specific to each participating  

[10:2:66,365] financial institution. CPCFA, the financial institution, and the borrower all contribute to fund the reserve. CPCFA provides  

[10:3:66,375] additional incentives for lending to businesses located in underserved communities and disaster areas. The reserve serves to  

[10:4:66,384] reduce loan-loss risk to the participating financial institutions and facilitates and fosters the relationship between the small  

[10:5:66,394] business and participating lenders.  

[11:0:66,411] Prior to 2010, CalCAP was entirely self-funded with $38 million transferred from CPCFA’s Small Business Assistance Fund  

[11:1:66,421] (SBAF), established with fees collected from large companies issuing debt through CPCFA's tax-exempt bond program. In  

[11:2:66,431] October 2010 CalCAP received $6 million from the state's General Fund (Chapter 731, Statutes of 2010) to increase lending  

[11:3:66,441] efforts. The $6 million appropriation was fully-expended by the end of 2018 allowing the annual recapture of contributions to  

[11:4:66,450] become the primary source for the State's contributions.  

[12:0:66,468] In 2011 California was awarded an allocation of federal funds in the amount of $168 million from the U.S. Treasury's State  

[12:1:66,478] Small Business Credit Initiative (SSBCI 1.0). CPCFA shared the allocation with the Infrastructure and Economic Development  

[12:2:66,487] Bank (IBank), now housed within the Governor's Office of Business and Economic Development (GO-Biz).  

[13:0:66,505] These funds enabled CPCFA to expand the availability of its CalCAP for Small Business (CalCAP SB). In addition, CPCFA  

[13:1:66,514] launched the Collateral Support Program (CSP) to further offset financial institution’s resistance to lend after the 2008  

[13:2:66,524] recession. CPCFA created the CSP with a recapture provision allowing for the sustainability of the program.  

[14:0:66,541] The federal government ended the SSBCI 1.0 program in 2017. At the request of the U.S. Treasury, CalCAP SB instituted an  

[14:1:66,551] annual recapture provision to provide sustainability beyond the active federal funding.  

[15:0:66,568] In 2021, the federal government reauthorized the funding of SSBCI (SSBCI 2.0). CPCFA and IBank partnered again to apply  

[15:1:66,578] for SSBCI 2.0 allocation and equally share the $1.181 billion allocated to California. Funding will be received in three tranches.  

[15:2:66,588] Recapture for the CalCAP SB program has been suspended for the SSBCI 2.0 program.  

[16:0:66,605] 0870 - CALIFORNIA RECYCLE UNDERUTILIZED SITES PROGRAM  

[17:0:66,623] The California Recycle Underutilized Sites (CALReUSE) program assisted with the reuse and redevelopment of underutilized  

[17:1:66,632] properties with real or perceived contamination issues. CALReUSE addressed a funding and information gap in the  

[17:2:66,642] development of contaminated properties (brownfields) to help bring these properties into productive reuse. Beginning in 2000,  

[17:3:66,652] the CALReUSE Assessment Program provided forgivable loans of up to $300,000 which was later increased to $500,000 per  

[17:4:66,662] brownfield site. The funds assisted with brownfield site assessment and characterization, technical assistance, and the  

[17:5:66,671] development of remedial action plans. Eligible projects included sites with potential beneficial reuse not currently redeveloped  

[17:6:66,681] due to lack of information about real or perceived contamination, uncertainty about clean-up costs, or concerns regarding time  

[17:7:66,691] frames and the regulatory process.  

[18:0:66,736] * Dollars in thousands, except in Salary Range. Numbers may not add or match to other statements due to rounding of budget details.

[3:0:66,68] Additionally, Proposition 1C, the Housing and Emergency Shelter Trust Fund Act of 2006, included provisions to fund  

[3:1:66,78] brownfield cleanup that resulted in the expansion of the CALReUSE Program. The CALReUSE Remediation Program received  

[3:2:66,88] $60 million in Proposition 1C funds and   provided loans and grants up to $5 million per project for brownfield remediation that  

[3:3:66,98] promoted affordable infill residential and mixed-used developments. Three projects were pre-approved to receive awards of  

[3:4:66,108] more than $5 million; one grant was increased above $5 million when returned funds became available. All CALReUSE  

[3:5:66,117] Program funds have been awarded and CPCFA continues to monitor and administer open grants to ensure compliance and  

[3:6:66,127] completion. The CALReUSE Program's $60 million investment in cleanup of contaminated sites in 30 California communities  

[3:7:66,137] has leveraged the completion of 2,310 housing units to date, of which 80% are affordable.  

[4:0:66,154] 0875 - ALTERNATIVE FINANCING PROGRAMS  

[5:0:66,174] Under its authority to provide alternative methods of financing facilities for pollution control, clean water supply and alternative  

[5:1:66,183] energy production, CPCFA is developing alternative financing models to assist sister agencies with achieving the state's  

[5:2:66,193] environmental goals. CPCFA is authorized to receive and accept contributions from other sources ("Independent Contributors")  

[5:3:66,203] including federal and state agencies to carry out the purposes of its statutory authority.  

[6:0:66,220] Using the Independent Contributor program and the CalCAP loan loss reserve model, the CPCFA partnered with the California  

[6:1:66,230] Air Resources Board (CARB) to assist truck and bus owners in meeting new clean air requirements by directing a total of  

[6:2:66,240] $215.9 million in Air Quality Improvement Program funds to incentivize lending for cleaner vehicles that comply with CARB's  

[6:3:66,250] Statewide Truck and Bus Rule. The CalCAP-CARB Heavy-Duty Vehicle Air Quality Loan Program ended on July 31, 2023.  

[6:4:66,259] CPCFA continues to partner with CARB to implement a zero-emissions loan loss reserve program.  

[7:0:66,277] CPCFA also partnered with the California Energy Commission to implement a $2 million pilot program to provide credit  

[7:1:66,286] enhancements and rebates to small business borrowers and multi-unit dwellings to install electric vehicle charging stations. 

[7:2:66,296] The CalCAP Electric Vehicle Charging Station (EVCS) Financing Program ended on March 31, 2022.  

[8:0:66,314] 0876 - CALIFORNIA CAPITAL ACCESS PROGRAM FOR AMERICANS WITH DISABILITIES  

[9:0:66,331] The objective of the California Capital Access program for Americans with Disabilities (CalCAP/ADA) created by Chapter 787,  

[9:1:66,341] Statutes of 2015, is to help small businesses comply with the federal Americans with Disabilities Act (ADA) and increase  

[9:2:66,350] access for   employees and customers by encouraging lenders and other financial institutions to make loans to very small  

[9:3:66,360] businesses to finance tenant improvements. The Legislature provided CPCFA an appropriation of $10 million effective January  

[9:4:66,370] 1, 2016, to administer a credit enhancement program following the CalCAP model   incentivizing lenders to enroll qualifying  

[9:5:66,380] loans.   The program also offers borrowers a rebate toward the cost of the Certified Access Specialist (CAS) Report.  

[10:0:66,397] 0877 - CALIFORNIA SEISMIC SAFETY CAPITAL ACCESS LOAN PROGRAM  

[11:0:66,416] The California Seismic Safety Capital Access Loan Program, authorized by Chapter 32, Statutes of 2016, provided a one-time  

[11:1:66,426] allocation of $10 million effective January 1, 2017 to administer a credit enhancement   program for   qualified small businesses  

[11:2:66,436] and residential property owners (including multi-unit dwellings and mobile homes registered by the California Department of  

[11:3:66,446] Housing and Community Development) for costs related to seismic retrofitting. The Program encourages banks and other  

[11:4:66,456] financial institutions to participate by creating loan loss reserve accounts for lenders that enroll qualifying loans into the  

[11:5:66,465] program.  

[12:0:66,483] 0878 - CALIFORNIA INVESTMENT AND INNOVATION PROGRAM  

[13:0:66,500] The California Investment and Innovation Program (Cal IIP) is authorized by Chapter 68, Statutes of 2022, (SB 193) and was  

[13:1:66,510] provided a one-time allocation of $50 million from the General Fund into a new special fund, the California Investment and  

[13:2:66,519] Innovation Fund, to support a new grant program to provide local assistance to the state’s Community Development Financial  

[13:3:66,529] Institutions (CDFIs). The Cal IIP is tasked with awarding up to $15 million annually, with a required award date of February 1,  

[13:4:66,539] with the initial award starting on February 1, 2024.

[12:0:154,567] 0977   California Health Facilities Financing Authority  

[13:0:66,587] The mission of the California Health Facilities Financing Authority is to help eligible and credit worthy nonprofit and public  

[13:1:66,597] health facilities reduce their cost of capital, and promote health care improvement and cost containment objectives by providing  

[13:2:66,607] cost-effective tax-exempt bond, low-cost loan, and direct grant programs.  

[13:3:66,624] The Authority was established by Chapter 1033, Statutes of 1979 (AB 1558), and consists of nine members: the State  

[13:4:66,634] Treasurer, the Director of the Department of Finance, the State Controller, two public members appointed by the Senate, two  

[13:5:66,644] public members appointed by the Assembly, and two public members appointed by the Governor.  

[14:0:66,736] * Dollars in thousands, except in Salary Range. Numbers may not add or match to other statements due to rounding of budget details.

[12:0:66,658] PROGRAM DESCRIPTIONS  

[12:1:66,677] 0880 - CHILDREN'S HOSPITAL PROGRAM  

[13:0:66,694] In 2004, California voters approved Proposition 61, which established the Children's Hospital Program. In 2008, Proposition 3  

[13:1:66,704] established the second Children's Hospital Program. In 2018, Proposition 4 established a third Children’s Hospital Program.  

[13:2:66,714] The purpose of the three programs is to improve the health and welfare of California's critically ill children by providing a stable  

[14:0:66,736] * Dollars in thousands, except in Salary Range. Numbers may not add or match to other statements due to rounding of budget details.

# === PAGE 233 === [size: 612x792]
[0:0:66,13] LEGISLATIVE, JUDICIAL, AND EXECUTIVE 

[1:0:410,13] 2024-25 STATE BUDGET — LJE   231 

[2:0:119,32] 0977   California Health Facilities Financing Authority - Continued 

[3:0:66,68] and ready source of funds for capital improvement projects for children's hospitals and, under Proposition 4, hospitals that  

[3:1:66,78] provide pediatric services for children eligible for California Children’s Services. Children's Hospital Program grant funding is  

[3:2:66,88] derived from the issuance of general obligation bonds, up to $750 million under Proposition 61, $980 million under Proposition  

[3:3:66,98] 3, and $1.5 billion under Proposition 4.  

[4:0:66,115] 0885 - HEALTH FACILITIES BONDS, LOANS, AND GRANTS  

[5:0:66,135] The Authority serves as a conduit provider of tax-exempt bonds and a direct provider of financial assistance to health  

[5:1:66,144] institutions.  

[6:0:66,164] Tax-Exempt Bond Program  

[7:0:66,183] Bonds issued under this conduit program are not a debt, liability, or a pledge of the full faith and credit of the taxing power of  

[7:1:66,193] the state or any of its political subdivisions. The full faith and credit of the participating institutions are pledged for repayment of  

[7:2:66,203] the bonds. To qualify for funding, the proposed project must be a health facility, operated by a private nonprofit corporation or  

[7:3:66,213] association, city, city and county, county, or hospital district.  

[8:0:66,232] HELP II Loan Program  

[9:0:66,250] The Authority administers the Healthcare Expansion Loan Program II (HELP II) to assist small and rural health facilities in  

[9:1:66,259] obtaining financing for their capital needs. The HELP II Program provides two percent and three percent fixed interest rate  

[9:2:66,269] loans of up to $2 million to California's nonprofit small and rural health facilities in an efficient, timely, and cost-effective manner.  

[9:3:66,279] HELP II loans may be used to purchase or construct new facilities, remodel or renovate existing facilities, purchase equipment  

[9:4:66,289] and/or furnishings, and refinance existing debt.  

[10:0:66,306] Distressed Hospital Loan Program  

[11:0:66,323] Chapter 6, Statutes of 2023 (AB 112) and Chapter 42, Statutes of 2023 (AB 118) establish the Distressed Hospital Loan  

[11:1:66,333] Program administered by the Department of Health Care Access and Information, in collaboration with the Authority, to provide  

[11:2:66,343] up to $300 million in interest-free, cashflow loans to not-for-profit hospitals and public hospitals in significant financial distress,  

[11:3:66,353] and certain closed hospitals.  

[12:0:66,370] Nondesignated Public Hospital Bridge Loan Program  

[13:0:66,387] The Nondesignated Public Hospital Bridge Loan Program provides cashflow loans to eligible nondesignated public hospitals  

[13:1:66,397] affected by the financial impacts of the COVID-19 public health emergency. These loans are required to be paid back within  

[13:2:66,407] two years and are secured by the borrower’s Medi-Cal reimbursements. The 2021 Budget Act included $40 million one-time  

[13:3:66,416] General Fund and the 2022 Budget Act included $40 million one-time General Fund to support this program.  

[14:0:66,434] Specialty Dental Clinic Grant Program  

[15:0:66,451] The Specialty Dental Clinic Grant Program provides competitive grants to eligible entities to support the construction,  

[15:1:66,461] expansion, modification, or adaptation of specialty dental clinics to increase access to oral health care for the special health  

[15:2:66,471] care needs populations.  

[16:0:66,488] 0890 - MENTAL HEALTH WELLNESS GRANTS  

[17:0:66,505] • Investment in Mental Health Wellness Act of 2013 Grant Program 

[18:0:66,522] Chapter 34, Statutes of 2013 (SB 82), the Investment in Mental Health Wellness Act of 2013 established a grant program to  

[18:1:66,532] disburse funds to California counties or to their nonprofit or public agency designees for the purpose of developing mental  

[18:2:66,542] health crisis support programs. Specifically, funds may be used to increase capacity for client assistance and services in crisis  

[18:3:66,552] intervention, crisis stabilization, crisis residential treatment, rehabilitative mental health services, and mobile crisis support  

[18:4:66,561] teams. The grants will support capital improvement, expansion and limited start-up costs.  

[19:0:66,581] • Investment in Mental Health Wellness Grant Program for Children and Youth  

[20:0:66,598] Chapter 30, Statutes of 2016 (SB 833), Section 20 established the Investment in Mental Health Wellness Grant Program for  

[20:1:66,608] Children and Youth to increase mental health services for children and youth 21 years of age and under to develop a complete  

[20:2:66,618] continuum of crisis services. Working with counties, the program funds facility acquisition, construction/renovation, equipment  

[20:3:66,628] acquisition, applicable startup or expansion costs, and limited personnel funding to develop crisis residential treatment and  

[20:4:66,637] crisis stabilization facilities, mobile crisis support teams, and family respite care facilities.  

[21:0:66,657] • Community Services Infrastructure Grants 

[22:0:66,674] Chapter 33, Statutes of 2016 (SB 843), Section 52 established the Community Services Infrastructure Grant Program to  

[22:1:66,684] expand community alternatives to jail and prison. The program seeks to expand access to jail and prison diversion programs  

[22:2:66,694] and services for those with mental health illness, substance use disorders, or who have suffered from trauma. Working with  

[22:3:66,704] counties, the program funds facility acquisition, renovation, equipment acquisition, and applicable startup or expansion costs for  

[22:4:66,713] facilities that provide services to this population.  

[23:0:66,736] * Dollars in thousands, except in Salary Range. Numbers may not add or match to other statements due to rounding of budget details.

[3:0:66,68] • No Place Like Home Program  

[4:0:66,86] Chapter 43, Statutes of 2016 (AB 1618) established the No Place Like Home (NPLH) Program, which requires the Department  

[4:1:66,96] of Housing and Community Development (HCD) to award up to $2 billion to counties for the acquisition, design, construction,  

[4:2:66,105] rehabilitation, or preservation of permanent supportive housing for individuals living with a severe mental illness who are  

[4:3:66,115] homeless or at risk of chronic homelessness. Chapter 322, Statutes of 2016 (AB 1628) authorized CHFFA to enter into  

[4:4:66,125] contracts with HCD and further authorized CHFFA to issue up to $2 billion in taxable revenue bonds pursuant to the NPLH  

[4:5:66,135] Program. Chapter 41, Statutes of 2018 (AB 1827) placed the NPLH Program on the November 2018 ballot (Proposition 2),  

[4:6:66,144] where it was adopted by the voters as the No Place Like Home Act. This ratified existing law establishing the NPLH Program  

[4:7:66,154] as being consistent with the Mental Health Services Act approved through Proposition 63 in 2004. It also ratified the issuance  

[4:8:66,164] of up to $2 billion in previously authorized bonds.

[9:0:211,388] 0981   California ABLE Act Board  

[9:1:66,409] The California Achieving a Better Life Experience Act (ABLE) Board   provides eligible individuals with disabilities the opportunity  

[9:2:66,419] to save private funds in tax-advantaged ABLE accounts for the purpose of supporting persons with disabilities to maintain their  

[9:3:66,428] health, independence, and quality of life.  

[10:0:66,448] The Board consists of the State Treasurer (Chair), the Director of Finance, the Controller, the Director of Developmental  

[10:1:66,458] Services, the Chairperson of the State Council on Developmental Disabilities, the Director of Rehabilitation, and the Chair of  

[10:2:66,467] the State Independent Living Council.
